    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

<span id="freeTextContainer1588849880358063952">Suor Teodora narra la storia di Agilulfo, cavaliere senza corpo, di cui vive solo l'armatura. Mentre Carlo Magno assedia Parigi, Agilulfo, dopo essersi coperto di gloria, decide di partire alla ricerca di Sofronia, fanciulla da lui salvata quindici anni prima.   <span id="freeText1588849880358063952" style="display:none">Suor Teodora narra la storia di Agilulfo, cavaliere senza corpo, di cui vive solo l'armatura. Mentre Carlo Magno assedia Parigi, Agilulfo, dopo essersi coperto di gloria, decide di partire alla ricerca di Sofronia, fanciulla da lui salvata quindici anni prima. Accompagnato dallo scudiero Gurdulù, attraverso numerose peripezie, inseguito dalla guerriera Bradamante innamorata di lui, Agilulfo riesce a trovare Sofronia, ma credendola macchiata di gravi peccati, decide di scomparire. Si sveste dell'armatura e la consegna a Rambaldo, giovane compagno d'armi. Sarà ora questi a proseguire nella bianca corazza, le gesta del cavaliere senza corpo.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1588849880358063952" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/35090310?book_show_action=true">Oct 11, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

              <span id="reviewTextContainer35090310" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9272484155808107077"><b>Il cavaliere inesistente = The Nonexistent Knight, Italo Calvino</b><br/><br/>The Nonexistent Knight is an allegorical fantasy novel by Italian writer Italo Calvino, first published in Italian in 1959 and in English translation in 1962. <br/><br/>The tale explores questions of identity, integration with society, and virtue through the adventures of Agilulf, a medieval knight who exemplifies chivalry, piety, and faithfulness but exists only as an empty suit of Armour.<br/><br/>تاریخ نخستین خوانش فارسی: روز هشتم ماه می سال 2002میل</span>
  <span id="freeText9272484155808107077" style="display:none"><b>Il cavaliere inesistente = The Nonexistent Knight, Italo Calvino</b><br /><br />The Nonexistent Knight is an allegorical fantasy novel by Italian writer Italo Calvino, first published in Italian in 1959 and in English translation in 1962. <br /><br />The tale explores questions of identity, integration with society, and virtue through the adventures of Agilulf, a medieval knight who exemplifies chivalry, piety, and faithfulness but exists only as an empty suit of Armour.<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش فارسی: روز هشتم ماه می سال 2002میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: شوالیه ی ناموجود؛ نویسنده: ایتالو کالوینو؛ مترجم: پرویز شهدی؛ تهران، چشمه، 1381؛ در 175ص؛ شابک 9643620514؛ چاپ سوم 1385؛ چاپ پنجم 1388؛ شابک 9789643620516؛ موضوع: داستانهای نویسندگان ایتالیایی - سده 20م<br /><br />ماجراهای جنگهای صلیبی است؛ «آژیلوف» شوالیه ای است که در سپاه «شارلمانی» «فرانسه» حضور دارد؛ او شوالیه ای منظم با زرهی سفید است، که در حقیقت جسم و وجود انسانی ندارد؛ یک اراده، و صدای انسانی درون زرهی فلزی، و با این حال دقیقتر و پرکارتر از سایر شوالیه هاست؛ به عبارتی شوالیه ای که وجود ندارد، اما حضور دارد ...؛ کتاب را دو سه بار خوانده ام، داستانهای «کالوینو» همیشه نازنین و خواندنی ست، همیشه بکر، به فکر دنبال کردن سرراست نباشید، «کالوینو» نوآور است؛ «رولان بارت»، نویسنده سده ی بیست میلادی و فرانسوی (نوشته هایش را جنابان آقایان پیام یزدانجو و احمد اخوت ترجمه کرده اند) «کالوینو» و «بورخس» را دو خط موازی میشمارند؛ و نیز «کالوینو» را نویسنده ای پست مدرن میدانند، چنان روشن همه چیز را به طنز میگیرند، و جهانی خلق میکنند سراسر از رمز و راز؛ تا بماند از ایشان یادگار و در همین روزگار ماها و همان روزگار که شمایان هستید، باشید تا هستی هست...؛ <br /><br />تاریخ بهنگام رسانی 23/07/1399هجری خورشیدی؛ ا. شربیانی</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2782405044" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2782405044" />
    <a title="Glenn Russell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23385697-glenn-russell"><img alt="Glenn Russell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1505195595p2/23385697.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2782405044?book_show_action=true">Apr 14, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Glenn Russell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Glenn Russell" href="/user/show/23385697-glenn-russell">Glenn Russell</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/19637099-the-nonexistent-knight">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2782405044" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11445590346088553830"><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1555020537i/27349926._SY540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Calvino whimsy and wizardry compressed into less than 150 pages. <br/><br/>Published in 1959, <i>The Nonexistent Knight</i> completes <i>Our Ancestors</i>, the Italian maestro's "heraldic trilogy" consisting also of <i>The Cloven Viscount</i> (1952) and <i>The Baron in the Trees</i> (1957). Beguiling, enchanting, quizzical; if retold in twenty pages as a Brothers Grim-style fairy tale, <i>The Nonexistent Knight</i> could instantly become a classic for all ages. Actually, in somewhat similar spirit, there's a charming Pino Zac animated film</span>
  <span id="freeText11445590346088553830" style="display:none"><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1555020537i/27349926._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Calvino whimsy and wizardry compressed into less than 150 pages. <br><br>Published in 1959, <i>The Nonexistent Knight</i> completes <i>Our Ancestors</i>, the Italian maestro's "heraldic trilogy" consisting also of <i>The Cloven Viscount</i> (1952) and <i>The Baron in the Trees</i> (1957). Beguiling, enchanting, quizzical; if retold in twenty pages as a Brothers Grim-style fairy tale, <i>The Nonexistent Knight</i> could instantly become a classic for all ages. Actually, in somewhat similar spirit, there's a charming Pino Zac animated film based on the novella. <br><br>The literary magic on display in <i>The Nonexistent Knight</i> is nothing short of breathtaking. We are in the time of Charlemagne during the war with the Turks and the central figure in the tale is a knight in shinning white armor by the name of Agilulf. But, let me add quickly, nobody is inside the white armor, hence the book's title. In the opening chapter, when passing inspection on leaders of his army, Emperor Charlemagne exclaims, "This is too much! We've even got a knight who doesn't exist!"<br><br>As one can very well imagine, being nonexistent does present its challenges: Agilulf thinks and acts in a number of curious ways, leaving a reader to ponder the philosophical implications of the paradoxes of existence or, er, nonexistence or . . . oh, my goodness, thinking about Agilulf in solely conceptual terms can quickly make one dizzy. No doubt about it, Agilulf is one knotty knight. But unknot we must, so let's shift to three specific conundrums surrounding our white-plated champion:<br><br>Pythagorean Paladin: Agilulf is faced with an ongoing dilemma: unless he keeps himself continually pitted against the challenges posed by the surrounding world, he might dissolve into nothingness. Thus, when not preoccupied by his knightly duties, he applies himself “to some precise exercise: counting objects, arranging them in geometric patterns, resolving problems of arithmetic.” In this respect, it appears Agilulf isn’t that much different than many men or women of action: an inability to let go of constantly calculating or analyzing; the frantic need to keep oneself busy, busy, busy. <br><br>Armor I Am: “For in times when armor was necessary even for a man who existed, how much more was it for one who didn’t.” Agilulf is one with his armor – no armor, no Agilulf. Throughout his tale, Italo Calvino has us reflect on what is lost when an individual’s identity is completely bound up with their social role and subsumed by their function within society. <br><br>To Sleep No More: When asked how he can keep awake at all times, Agilulf replies: “I would feel bewildered if I dozed off for even a second. In fact I’d never come round at all but would be lost forever. So I keep wide awake every second of the day and night.” That is different! No sleep for Agilulf. We humans thrive on sleep, especially the restful state of deep, dreamless sleep. Our brains require sleep, thus in a twenty-four hour cycle, we don’t mind surrendering our sense of self-identity to the selfless state of sleep. In his current condition of nonexistence, even if for one instant Agilulf surrenders to the selfless state of sleep, Agilulf risks losing whatever nonexistent existence he has. Poor knight! Keep on arranging your pine cones into triangles, Agilulf, we wouldn’t want to lose you. <br><br>Alas, we are not reading a philosophic treatise in the field of ontology or metaphysics but a tale of the fantastic written by none other than Italo Calvino. Thus we can move along and meet several other characters making their appearance in the story: <br><br>Raimbaut: A youth wishing to join the ranks of Charlemagne's paladins to avenge the death of his father slain by the head of the Turks. Little does this young man know where his adventures will take him. Hint: many the time his path crosses that of the knight in shinning white armor. <br><br>Gurduloo: One of the names of this rambunctious chap who exists but has no sense of self, the exact opposite of Agilulf, the knight who doesn’t exist but has an extremely strong sense of self. We first meet Gurduloo waddling along, a duck among ducks in his earthen color rags. As we come to learn, Gurduloo is but one name for this foolish fellow who takes the temporary identity of whatever he’s around. In the Tibetan Buddhist tradition, Gurduloo might be judged as possessing crazy wisdom but in Medieval Europe he’s considered just plain crazy. But then again, not so fast: we have the paradox of the wise fool. Any guesses as to who gets to be Agilulf's squire?<br><br>Brandamante: Bold, courage, accomplished knight who just so happens to be a woman. This female warrior is none too pleased with all the "regular" knights she thinks are nothing but a bunch of louts, nincompoops and slobs. But there is that one knight who alone knows the secret geometry, the order, the rule, by which to bestow sense to her every action, nay, to her very life itself. Agilulf to the rescue! Well, maybe. <br><br>The Tale's Narrator: "Book, evening is here, and I have begun to write more rapidly. . . . Even now and again I notice my pen beginning to hurry over the paper as if by itself, with my hurrying along after it." So writes Sister Theodora, a nun living in her convent. The interplay of knightly action and the act of writing gives <i>The Nonexistent Knight</i> a touch of the metafictional, a small preview of what Italo Calvino would develop in his famous 1979 novel alternating story with the act of reading a book. <br><br>Torrismund, Sophronia, Palmyra: Three more players whose ultimate identity I will leave to each reader to discover. <br><br>Knights of the Grail: Although not quite as off-kilter as the Pythons' film of King Arthur and the Holy Grail, <i>The Nonexistent Knight</i> does have its moments. Perhaps the consequence of the author playing with themes from Ariosto's <i>Orlando Furioso</i> and other classics of medieval epic literature. One can almost not believe how Italo Calvino has those idealized champions behave. To discover what is meant here and all else that's completely different, you will have to read <i>The Nonexistent Knight</i> for yourself.<br><br>Coda: Italo Calvino chose the above Picasso drawing to illustrate the cover for the first published edition of <i>The Nonexistent Knight</i>.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1555247798i/27362924._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Italian author Italo Calvino, 1923-1985<br><br>“If a girl has had enough of every man who exists, her only remaining desire could be for a man who doesn't exist at all.”<br>― Italo Calvino, <i>The Nonexistent Knight</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="11445590346088553830" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

<span id="freeTextContainer13124136874846656075">Calvino is the man. This little book is a story about story-telling, but really it's about Life and Being. And it's actually fun and funny! I think I'm gonna re-read it right away.<br/>As always, story, structure and theme are one. This is the way it's done Larsson! (sorry, had to get that dig in, even though the poor guy is dead (Larsson I mean), and I just read that all he did was drink coffee and smoke, which explains a lot (see my review of his book)...)<br/><br/>Anyway if you want a fun little medieval ad</span>
  <span id="freeText13124136874846656075" style="display:none">Calvino is the man. This little book is a story about story-telling, but really it's about Life and Being. <div id="review_3328757430" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3328757430" />
    <a title="Matthew Appleton" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/57003346-matthew-appleton"><img alt="Matthew Appleton" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1578773686p2/57003346.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3328757430?book_show_action=true">May 11, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matthew Appleton" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matthew Appleton" href="/user/show/57003346-matthew-appleton">Matthew Appleton</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780544959101" href="/book/show/30971657-the-nonexistent-knight">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/57003346-matthew-appleton?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/57003346-matthew-appleton?shelf=foreign-translated">foreign-translated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/57003346-matthew-appleton?shelf=read-2020">read-2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/57003346-matthew-appleton?shelf=italian-literature">italian-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3328757430" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10749060116094029949">74th book of 2020. <br/><br/>The final book of Calvino's triptych <i>Our Ancestors</i>, and though pales slightly in the wake of the second book - The Baron in the Trees (<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2662551348' rel='nofollow noopener'>review here</a>) - this was still an entertaining, bizarre read. <br/><br/>As the title suggests, Agilulf is 'non-existent'; he is simply a suit of armour. His squire, Gurduloo, also doesn't know if he exists, a rather madman, who, when introduced in the story, is chasing ducks in a lake believing he might be one himself. The book is only just over 100 pages</span>
  <span id="freeText10749060116094029949" style="display:none">74th book of 2020. <br /><br />The final book of Calvino's triptych <i>Our Ancestors</i>, and though pales slightly in the wake of the second book - The Baron in the Trees (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2662551348" rel="nofollow noopener">review here</a>) - this was still an entertaining, bizarre read. <br /><br />As the title suggests, Agilulf is 'non-existent'; he is simply a suit of armour. His squire, Gurduloo, also doesn't know if he exists, a rather madman, who, when introduced in the story, is chasing ducks in a lake believing he might be one himself. The book is only just over 100 pages but reads quite slowly. The writing is good, but again, not at the same beauty Calvino captures in the prior story, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9804.The_Baron_in_the_Trees" title="The Baron in the Trees by Italo Calvino" rel="nofollow noopener">The Baron in the Trees</a>.<br /><br />Some of Gurduloo's parts reveal story points so I'll only write this one in, which captures the madness and humour of his character. He is given a vat of soup:<br /><br /><i>'All is soup!' resounded his voice from inside the vat, which tipped over at his onslaught.<br />Gurduloo was now imprisoned in the overturned pot. His spoon could be heard banging like a cracked bell, and his voice moaning, 'All is soup!' Then the vat moved like a tortoise, turned over again, and Gurduloo reappeared.</i><br /><br />Surely that's enough reason to read this short novella alone.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10749060116094029949" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer8653397129206384785">I get it, and what I get is done well: it's a parody of those Sir Gawain and Perceval medieval knight narratives, and it sticks delightfully and ironically true to form. The point, though, about knights being slave to their codes, is pretty basic and didn't need a full novella to get across. This could've been an equally (actually, significantly more) effective vignette. Meh. </span>

<div id="review_3405200443" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3405200443" />
    <a title="Uhtred" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/109743598-uhtred"><img alt="Uhtred" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1581262401p2/109743598.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3405200443?book_show_action=true">Jun 22, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Uhtred" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Uhtred" href="/user/show/109743598-uhtred">Uhtred</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3405200443" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10335869699785855884">What a worthy conclusion! The Nonexistent Knight is the third book of the trilogy of Our ancestors, after the Cloven Viscount and the Baron on the trees, which I have reread all together after more than 20 years.<br/>The plot is set at the time of the Crusades and winds around two characters: Agilulf, a knight made of an empty armor, all conscience and willpower, and Gurdulù, with a material body but devoid of conscience, which will become in the development of the history, the squire of Agilulf. Aro</span>
  <span id="freeText10335869699785855884" style="display:none">What a worthy conclusion! The Nonexistent Knight is the third book of the trilogy of Our ancestors, after the Cloven Viscount and the Baron on the trees, which I have reread all together after more than 20 years.<br />The plot is set at the time of the Crusades and winds around two characters: Agilulf, a knight made of an empty armor, all conscience and willpower, and Gurdulù, with a material body but devoid of conscience, which will become in the development of the history, the squire of Agilulf. Around these two characters, one without physical body and the other without conscience, orbit the other characters, among which stands out Rambaldo, who is almost another protagonist. At the beginning of the book we find Agilulf at the court of Charlemagne in Paris, where he is not at all likeable to the other knights, since he is too perfect and inflexible. And we find also Rambaldo, who is trying to take revenge on the pagan Isoarre, who killed his father. He succeeds, but falls into an ambush and is saved by a mysterious knight, who later turns out to be the beautiful Bradamante, whom he obviously falls in love with; but unfortunately Bradamante is in love with Agilulfo, and therefore refuses the advances of Rambaldo. And so far the story still seems almost normal. The story rears up when Torrismondo, a boy of about fifteen years old, reveals that he is the son of Sofronia, a woman who, saved about fifteen years earlier by Agilulf from the hands of some evildoers and believed to be virgin, had brought to Agilulf the title of Knight because he had defended her illibacy. Agilulf therefore feels he is a Knight for an invalid reason and goes into crisis, because a perfect man of honor like him cannot live with this shame. He then sets off in search of Sofronia to discover the truth, followed by Bradamante, who loves Agilulf, and Rambaldo, who loves Bradamante, with Torrismondo who instead takes another road, because he wants to find his father, who has joined the "Sacred Order of the Knights of the Grail ".<br />Agilulfo goes to Morocco in the footsteps of Sofronia, while Torrismondo goes to Curvaldia, where he discovers that the Knights of the Grail are not paladins at all, but they are tyrants who harass the farmers with heavy taxes. After a number of adventures, Agilulf finds Sofronia and brings her to the Franks' camp; in the meantime Torrismondo discovers that Sofronia is not his mother, but almost his half-sister, since he is the son of the Queen of Scotland and a knight of the Sacred Order, while she is the daughter of the King of Scotland and a peasant woman; whereupon the two fall in love, are married by Charlemagne and then become the sovereigns of Curvaldia.<br />These developments of the story, however, do not reach Agilulf, who, believing he has lost his honor, decides to commit suicide, gives his armor to Rambaldo and thus vanishes. But there is also time for another twist: Rambaldo, with Agilulfo's shiny armor, meets with Bradamante who, mistaking him for the Non-existent Knight, gives in to his flattery, but as soon as the misunderstanding is discovered, she decides to return to the cloistered life in the convent. In the final part of the book, Rambaldo goes to the monastery to convince Bradamante to escape with him. To narrate all this intricate story is a nun, Sister Teodora, who only in the end reveals her true identity, that is to be Bradamante. What a mess!<br />Like the other two books in the trilogy, this too can be read at the level of the basic story: fun, intriguing, full of humorous twists. But Calvino's vein is very recognizable, never without the deepest meanings: here we go in search of individuality. What is individuality? What sets us apart, of course. And for someone it is honor, for someone ethics, for someone will, for someone stubbornness, for someone war, for someone love; all concepts found in these characters, who entrust them with the proof of their existence. But Agilulf is the nonexistent knight, he does not exist, and therefore he is devoid of identity, he is almost invisible to everyone. It is empty, like its armor, and stands only for its great willpower. How many men like this do we know?</span>
  <a data-text-id="10335869699785855884" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15185047990032186377">“I'm talking to you, paladin!" insisted Charlemagne. "Why don't you show your face to your king?"⁣⁣<br/>A voice came clearly through the gorge piece. "Sire, because I do not exist!"⁣⁣<br/>"And how do you do your job, then, if you don't exist?"⁣⁣<br/>"By will power," said Agilulf, "and faith in our holy cause!"⁣⁣<br/>⁣⁣<br/>The story evolves around our nonexistent knight, Agilulf Emo Bertrandin of the Guildivern and of the Others of Corbentraz and Sura. He is the Emperor Charlemagne's most dedicated knight. He is righteo</span>
  <span id="freeText15185047990032186377" style="display:none">“I'm talking to you, paladin!" insisted Charlemagne. "Why don't you show your face to your king?"⁣⁣<br />A voice came clearly through the gorge piece. "Sire, because I do not exist!"⁣⁣<br />"And how do you do your job, then, if you don't exist?"⁣⁣<br />"By will power," said Agilulf, "and faith in our holy cause!"⁣⁣<br />⁣⁣<br />The story evolves around our nonexistent knight, Agilulf Emo Bertrandin of the Guildivern and of the Others of Corbentraz and Sura. He is the Emperor Charlemagne's most dedicated knight. He is righteous, perfectionist, faithful and pious knight. Agilulf exists only to obey and execute rules, protocols, and systems of knighthood.⁣⁣<br />⁣⁣<br />On the contrary, there is his squire, who has many names that change according to season and place: Gurdulu, Gudi-Ussif, Ben-Va-Ussuf, Ben Stanbul, Pestanzul, Bertinzul, Martinbon, Martinzul, Omobon, Omobestia, Il Brutto del Vallone, Gian Paciasso, Pier Paciugo, Boamoluz, Carotun, Balingaccio, and Bertella. In short, "he is a man without a name and with every possible name."⁣⁣<br />⁣⁣<br />Gurduloo thinks everything backward and when given a tin of soup, he puts his head into it unaware that he must eat the soup and not be eaten by the soup. The two of them represent the nonexistent but conscious individual and the existent but unconscious individual.⁣⁣<br />⁣⁣<br />"That's a good one! We have a subject who exists but doesn't realize he does and there's my paladin who thinks he exists but actually doesn't. They'd make a great pair, let me tell you!"⁣⁣<br />⁣⁣<br />The plot twists when his knighthood is being challenged by a young warrior, Torrismund. And his love interest, Sister Theodora, satirises Agilulf as the ideal man who could live only in one's imagination.⁣⁣<br />⁣⁣<br />The story makes me contemplate on my own existence and conscience.⁣<br />⁣<br />"World conditions were still confused in the era when this took place. It was not rare then to find names and thoughts and forms and institutions that corresponded to nothing in existence."⁣⁣</span>

<div id="review_732341240" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/732341240" />
    <a title="Bjorn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19667916-bjorn"><img alt="Bjorn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1367928200p2/19667916.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/732341240?book_show_action=true">Oct 02, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bjorn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bjorn" href="/user/show/19667916-bjorn">Bjorn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19667916-bjorn?shelf=italy">italy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer732341240" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11800866117948673031">What starts out as a straight tale (and yes, there's an in-story storyteller telling us that they're telling us a tale) of a bunch of knights in one of Charlemagne's campaigns against the muslims gets thrown off its track when it turns out that one of the knights, the wisest, fairest, chastest, most analytically-minded of them all, doesn't actually exist; he's just an empty suit of armour held together by the idea of his own existence. So Charlemagne, in his wisdom, gives him a Sancho Panza-styl</span>
  <span id="freeText11800866117948673031" style="display:none">What starts out as a straight tale (and yes, there's an in-story storyteller telling us that they're telling us a tale) of a bunch of knights in one of Charlemagne's campaigns against the muslims gets thrown off its track when it turns out that one of the knights, the wisest, fairest, chastest, most analytically-minded of them all, doesn't actually exist; he's just an empty suit of armour held together by the idea of his own existence. So Charlemagne, in his wisdom, gives him a Sancho Panza-style squire in the shape of a madman who exists, but isn't aware of it, expecting them to get on well with each other. And off we go.<br /><br />As with most of Calvino's books, it's hard to describe it without killing what makes it come alive. We have a group of characters who are really just philosophical concepts dressed up as knights and taken so far to their respective extremes that they barely work, blundering through the plots of Candide and Monty Python's Holy Grail, trying to work out whether they exist or not. And since they're philosophical concepts, it gets meta as fuck... but it never gets annoyingly, preciously so, partly because at just over 100 pages it never has time to, but also because he knows how to riff on tales we've heard before (and does so far better than in Castle of Crossed Destinies IMO). And somehow, after all these high concept modernist ideas fighting each other, he comes out with a rather beautiful ending in praise of the possibilities of fiction, of everything that doesn't exist.<br /><br /><i>But now, o future, I'm in the saddle and riding your horse. What new flags do you raise at me from the towers in a city yet to be founded? What fires will you let ravage the castles and gardens I've loved? What unforeseen golden ages do you have lined up for me, you unbending, you bearer of treasures to be dearly paid for, you my country to be conquered, you future...</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="11800866117948673031" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_734146363" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/734146363" />
    <a title="Erika" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4532400-erika"><img alt="Erika" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1523323123p2/4532400.jpg" /></a>

Well, I didn’t like it. I really couldn't care about any of the characters on this book. To me, it felt like the story was all over the place, there was no clear motives, no structure. And the names, I really hated the names of everything, so nonsensical. Not my kind of literature at all.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer3539891375498590939">3.5 star.<br/>Read this and you will understand why this book was listed in the set Our Ancestors. When I read this book, I can feel the air, the setting of the old age, how people used to think, to believe, and especially, their rotten behavior surrounding a knight that devoted his life, his being, his existence for order, honor, duty.   <span id="freeText3539891375498590939" style="display:none">3.5 star.<br />Read this and you will understand why this book was listed in the set Our Ancestors. When I read this book, I can feel the air, the setting of the old age, how people used to think, to believe, and especially, their rotten behavior surrounding a knight that devoted his life, his being, his existence for order, honor, duty. He acted upon his codes strictly and would like everyone else to follow him exactly. That was one of the reasons why he was not loved much among the army of the King, but he didn't care about, just like a truly noble knight. Even the most challenging task: to overcome the trap of women, of corruption, a trap that had caught so many other nobles, could not set his back on his journey. And he even changed the woman who set the trap (amazing, btw). :)))<br />His fate, on the other hand, I believe it to be unfair, but probably that was what had happened, not only to him but also to all of the people like him, and in general, his beliefs, his spirit. One can only hope that he went to a better place, and the inheritance of his armor was a sign that his glory would pass for the next generation and maybe developed to a new level.<br />The drama and plot twist in this book was fun too. It's not much related to the main plot, but it was good. The twist was like 3 slaps in the face of the reader, in a row, immediately, and it left you speechless. There was also a small revelation at the end of the book about why the narrator was so familiar with the lives that she was never acquainted with. And after all, living happily ever after for everyone, except, you know, our Knight.</span>

<div id="review_2530064860" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2530064860" />
    <a title="Robert" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/234027-robert"><img alt="Robert" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1519321845p2/234027.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2530064860?book_show_action=true">Sep 20, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robert" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robert" href="/user/show/234027-robert">Robert</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/19637099-the-nonexistent-knight">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2530064860" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16055364666993234739">I love Italo Calvino. Before I ever read Calvino, I was a big fan of Donald Barthelme, and I mistakenly thought that Barthelme had forged a new kind of fiction, a hilarious, cutting breed of metafiction that had no precedent. Little did I know, at the time, how much Barthelme had drawn on earlier writers like Calvino. For that matter, he drew heavily on many other writers, I now know, going through the absurdists and going back through time. Even Don Quixote contains surprisingly modern-seeming </span>
  <span id="freeText16055364666993234739" style="display:none">I love Italo Calvino. Before I ever read Calvino, I was a big fan of Donald Barthelme, and I mistakenly thought that Barthelme had forged a new kind of fiction, a hilarious, cutting breed of metafiction that had no precedent. Little did I know, at the time, how much Barthelme had drawn on earlier writers like Calvino. For that matter, he drew heavily on many other writers, I now know, going through the absurdists and going back through time. Even Don Quixote contains surprisingly modern-seeming metafiction elements. But in the modern era, it is Calvino who is the true king of metafiction.<br /><br />The Nonexistent Knight is about just what it says it's about: a knight in shining armor whose armor is literally empty, because he doesn't exist. The knight is merely some kind of trick of force of will that allows armor to stand and move and speak. <br /><br />Amusingly, the nonexistent knight is a total prick, a stickler for the rules. Not having a body, he doesn't need to eat or sleep (can't do either, in fact), and so when he's not fighting or saving damsels in distress, he's performing the numerous administrative functions of nobility. In this way, Calvino manages to skewer both chivalry and bureaucratic mundanity with a single joke.<br /><br />As is typical with Calvino, the story contains many absurd elements. For all that, however, it's moving and earnest and heartfelt. It's not merely an extended joke. What is it about? Love, mostly. But also officiousness, violence, brutality, religion, belief, class, gender, and so many other things. Well worth the read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16055364666993234739" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_3502882387" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3502882387" />
    <a title="Indran Fernando" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/30920756-indran-fernando"><img alt="Indran Fernando" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1463204281p2/30920756.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3502882387?book_show_action=true">Aug 24, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Indran Fernando" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Indran Fernando" href="/user/show/30920756-indran-fernando">Indran Fernando</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/19637099-the-nonexistent-knight">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer3502882387&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3502882387" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9059393504610792250">I began this novella knowing only that it's about a talking, moving suit of armor and that some describe the work as "allegorical." The most pleasant surprise turned out to be that, as was the case with Calvino's Baron in the Trees, the allegorical aspects are not heavy handed at all. There is room for every reader to wonder, "What is the underlying message here?" and arrive at a widely varying, even contradictory, conclusions.<br/><br/>There is also room for the reader to set aside that question and simp</span>
  <span id="freeText9059393504610792250" style="display:none">I began this novella knowing only that it's about a talking, moving suit of armor and that some describe the work as "allegorical." The most pleasant surprise turned out to be that, as was the case with Calvino's Baron in the Trees, the allegorical aspects are not heavy handed at all. There is room for every reader to wonder, "What is the underlying message here?" and arrive at a widely varying, even contradictory, conclusions.<br /><br />There is also room for the reader to set aside that question and simply appreciate aspects like the following:<br />-poetic descriptions spanning all five senses;<br />-the rushes of multiple emotions all at once; and<br />-the thought-provoking tangents taken by the narrator, who pauses mid-scene to muse about some tidbit of an idea.<br /><br />Here's an example of what I mean by poetic descriptions: "Amid the usual confusion of horses, the forest of lances rippled into waves like a corn field moved by the passing wind."<br /><br />Multifaceted emotional states were one of the most striking characteristics of Baron in the Trees. Here's one of the most memorable examples from The Nonexistent Knight:<br />"The emotion at seeing Isohar dead on the ground, contradictory thoughts assailing him--of triumph at being able to finally say his father's blood was avenged, of doubt whether *he* had actually killed the Argalif by fracturing his spectacles and so could consider the vendetta duly consummated, of confusion at finding himself suddenly deprived of the aim which had brought him so far--all lasted only a moment. Then he felt a wonderful sense of lightness finding himself rid of that nagging thought in the middle of battle, and able to rush about, look round, fight, as if his feet had wings."<br /><br />As for the narrator's tangential musings, I hesitate to give an example so that you can experience them for yourself. Suffice it to say that Calvino has the good taste to keep these interjections succinct, with a "distilled" quality. Unlike most writers who implement such asides, Calvino never lets them wear out their welcome or encumber the unfolding of the plot. He sprinkles throughout the narration small gems of ruminations.<br /><br />Here's other stuff that I liked:<br /><br />-The unfolding relationship between the narrator and the narration and the "narratees"<br /><br />-The idea that for all our hatred of bureaucracies, deep down we subconsciously crave the sense of order that they provide--because we are terrified by the all-consuming chaos of the universe. To use Calvino's metaphor, we spend our lives struggling to convince ourselves that we are floating in the soup, as opposed to just being an indistinguishable region of soup within its endless, uniform expanse... To achieve status of some kind (e.g., a military rank or title of nobility) creates the sense that one's life has meaning--what is that but an illusion? On what basis can we claim that we exist at all and that the titular knight does not?<br /><br />-Is the writer a participant in the world or an observer-from-afar? The narrator's ambiguous, evolving role in the narration explores the tension between living life (i.e. leaving her convent) versus staying indoors to write *about* it.<br /><br />-A hilarious, satirical depiction of war which brings to mind Monty Python and the Holy Grail, or the Princess Bride. The whimsical exploration of knighthood/chivalry may bear some similarities to Don Quixote, but I'll find out for sure in 2021.<br /><br />-Exploration of the murky relationship between the mind and the body.<br /><br />-An exploration of the concept of will power. On the one hand there is the Nonexistent Knight's absolute will power, such that his existence consists *only* of a righteous sense of duty, with no capacity for emotion, no need to eat or drink or sleep, and not even a physical body! In contrast, there is the vagabond Gurduloo, who has no will power at all, as well as young Raimbaut, who is smarter than Gurduloo but ultimately still controlled by his emotions (first, the desire to avenge his dead father, later passionate love). And yet a third case is the Knights of the Holy Grail, who cede all will and desires to the idol-like Grail, which moves them around like puppets and guides them to victory on the battlefield. Calvino doesn't just flesh out these archetypes, he collides them with each other in amusing ways.<br /><br />-A notion which appeared in the final story of Calvino's Invisible Cities: that the people who purport to be the most morally righteous might actually be just as barbaric and authoritarian as unabashed evil-doers. I think this can't hurt to keep in mind.<br /><br />-Exploration of ideas related to romance. 1) The more unattainable and disinterested someone is, the more attractive we find him/her. 2) To fall in love with someone is to invent a fictional character. 3) Romance is more narcissistic than we care to admit (this reminded me of the passage in Infinite Jest dealing with people's preference for voice calls over video so as to be able to look at themselves in the mirror).<br /><br />I'm tempted to give it 5 stars, but it didn't feel perfect. Gurdoloo's antics started to wear thin, and the part where he road a turtle across the sea tipped the scales too far in the whimsical direction. (Note: the protagonist walking from one continent to another via the bottom of the ocean was fucking hilarious though.) <br /><br />Comparing this work with other stuff that I've read by Calvino, I might say the following:<br /><br />-It's more succinct than Baron, while nevertheless bringing up a wider range of ideas, like Cosmicomics. On an emotional level, this novella didn't pull me in as much as Baron, because the characters were less developed (I suppose that's one reason why people throw around the term "allegorical"). Even though there are similar amounts of heartbreak in both works, I didn't sympathize with the characters as much in this one.<br /><br />-It may be a little bit funnier than Invisible Cities, but not quite as funny as Cosmicomics.<br /><br />Note: I listened to this as an audiobook while staring at the highway for 4 hours, which was a more passive experience compared to reading (I like to jot LMFAoOoO!!!!! all over the margins like a maniac, etc). I suspect that reading this, or even listening to it again, will lead me to bump it up to 5 stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9059393504610792250" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1883085623" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1883085623" />
    <a title="Chris" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/854878-chris"><img alt="Chris" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1485122534p2/854878.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1883085623?book_show_action=true">Mar 20, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris" href="/user/show/854878-chris">Chris</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1883085623" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17548656492666898033">Calvino is a canny writer of fantasy. What I love is that the title tells all: this novella really is the tale of a knight who does not exist. When we first meet this knight prominent among Charlemagne's paladins, no character seems especially surprised that there is a brave and meticulous knight who emptily animates a white set of armor and a series of absurdly chivalric deeds. It's only as the picaresque comedy snowballs that Calvino's metafictions and politics come to the fore. We begin the n</span>
  <span id="freeText17548656492666898033" style="display:none">Calvino is a canny writer of fantasy. What I love is that the title tells all: this novella really is the tale of a knight who does not exist. When we first meet this knight prominent among Charlemagne's paladins, no character seems especially surprised that there is a brave and meticulous knight who emptily animates a white set of armor and a series of absurdly chivalric deeds. It's only as the picaresque comedy snowballs that Calvino's metafictions and politics come to the fore. We begin the novel startled by the ways its characters remain nonplussed before the unexplained nonexistence of Agilulfo. Only toward the end of his adventures do we see how many of our assumptions about political hierarchy and practical philosophy function as beliefs in nonexistence. A lot of uncomfortable laughter in this read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17548656492666898033" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer16177563794738743410">Growing up, at school we had to learn all Italian authors, and one that I could not stand was Italo Calvino. I still don’t know why, perhaps because I didn’t like to read at that time, but I found Calvino incredibly boring and hard to understand. In high school we started talking about Calvino, his life and works and the first book we had to read was “Il cavaliere Inesistente”. <br/>I have never loved a book this much, it attracted me instantly and I immediately started regretting not reading it when</span>
  <span id="freeText16177563794738743410" style="display:none">Growing up, at school we had to learn all Italian authors, and one that I could not stand was Italo Calvino. I still don’t know why, perhaps because I didn’t like to read at that time, but I found Calvino incredibly boring and hard to understand. In high school we started talking about Calvino, his life and works and the first book we had to read was “Il cavaliere Inesistente”. <br />I have never loved a book this much, it attracted me instantly and I immediately started regretting not reading it when I first had the chance. And now I think I needed to grow a little older in order to like and understand his writing and the plot. An amazing book that certainly did not disappoint me. </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2551129768" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2551129768" />
    <a title="Adam " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28230750-adam"><img alt="Adam " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1420275025p2/28230750.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2551129768?book_show_action=true">Oct 14, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Adam " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Adam " href="/user/show/28230750-adam">Adam </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/19637099-the-nonexistent-knight">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28230750-adam?shelf=chanson-de-geste">chanson-de-geste</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28230750-adam?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28230750-adam?shelf=italy">italy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2551129768" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer846763996503287109"><i>It was the hour in which objects lose the consistency of shadow that accompanies them during the night and gradually reacquire colors, but seem to cross meanwhile an uncertain limbo, faintly touched, just breathed on by light; the hour in which one is least certain of the world’s existence.</i><br/><br/><i>The art of writing tales consists in an ability to draw the rest of life from the nothing one has understood of it, but life begins again at the end of the page when one realises that one knew nothing whatsoev</i></span>
  <span id="freeText846763996503287109" style="display:none"><i>It was the hour in which objects lose the consistency of shadow that accompanies them during the night and gradually reacquire colors, but seem to cross meanwhile an uncertain limbo, faintly touched, just breathed on by light; the hour in which one is least certain of the world’s existence.</i><br /><br /><i>The art of writing tales consists in an ability to draw the rest of life from the nothing one has understood of it, but life begins again at the end of the page when one realises that one knew nothing whatsoever.</i><br /><br />Really liked it. You can tell Italo Calvino has a lot of respect for Ariosto and the tradition of the gestes (I think Cesare Pavese said Italo re-read the Furioso once a year). Both in direct references to them, and in borrowing two of Ariosto's best tricks: calling attention to the artificiality of the tale by creating a metanarrative about the writer's job of guiding the tale along, and creating humour by contrasting the modern world with that of the knights, in this case by creating an elaborate bureaucracy for the paladins. (On a similar note, just the other day I was reading a bit of the Orlandino, I forget if it was Arieto's or Folengo's, it was just something I found online in Italian and pasted into google translate for the ease of my shitty, monolingual, worm-ridden anglo brain but one of the first stanzas talks about how in the old chansons there's no modern renaissance versimilitude, and the knights sleep without removing their boots and armour). <br /><br />There are two small criticisms I'll make, though: first, that sort of Monty Python style medieval humour always bugged me. It makes everything into such a farce that you don't care about the characters. Italo Calvino comes very close to having that happen here. (Douglas Adams is the same for me except in the SF genre). Second, there's a sort of unpolished nature to it that leaves the ending feeling a bit rushed. Although it redeems itself because the nun writer herself admits and calls attention to it.<br /><br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[“That’s quite simple, my lad,” said Agilulf, and there was a certain warmth in his voice, the warmth of one who knows rules and regulations by heart and enjoys showing his own competence, and confusing other’s ignorance. “You must put in a request to the Superintendency of Duels, Feuds and Besmirched Honor, specifying the motives for your request and it will then be considered how to best place you in a position to attain the satisfaction you desire.” <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[“I’ll search out Isohar and gut him! Him and him alone!”<br />“You’ll end in the guardhouse, not in battle, you can be sure of that! Just think a little before speaking. If we make difficulties about Isohar, there are reasons. Suppose our emperor, for instance, is in the middle of negotiations with Isohar?”<br />But one of the officials whose head had been buried in papers till then now raised it jubilantly. “All solved! All solved! No need to do a thing! No point in a vendetta here! The other day Oliver thought two of his uncles were killed in battle and avenged them! But they’d stayed behind and got drunk under a table! We have these two extra uncles’ vendettas on our hands, a terrible mess. Now it can all be settled. We count an uncle’s vendetta as half a father’s. It’s as if we had a father’s vendetta clear, already carried out” <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ Amid incomprehensible words and mistakes, the meaning of what he said was more or less, “I touch my nose with the earth. I fall to my feet at your knees. I declare myself an august servant of your most humble majesty. Order and I will obey myself!” He brandished a spoon tied to his belt “And when your majesty says, ‘I order command and desire,’ and do this with your scepter, as I do, with this, d’you see? And when you shout as I shout, ‘I orderrr commanddd and desirrrre!’ you subjects must all obey me or I’ll have you strung up, you first there with that beard and silly old face.” <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />Reminded me of A Field in England: <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[As Agilulf dragged a corpse along he thought, “Oh corpse, you have what I never had or will have: a carcass. Or rather you have, you are this carcass, that which at times, in moments of despondency, I find myself envying in men who exist. Fine! I can truly call myself privileged, I who can live without it and do all; all, of course, which seems most important to me. Many things I manage to do better than those who exist, since I lack their usual defects of coarseness, carelessness, incoherence, smell. It’s true that someone who exists always has a particular attitude of his own to things, which I never managed to have. But if their secret is merely here, in this bag of guts, then I can do without it. This valley of disintegrating naked corpses disgusts me no more than the flesh of living human beings.”<br />As Gurduloo dragged a corpse along he thought “Corpsey, your farts stink even more than mine. I don’t know why everyone mourns you so. What’s it you lack? Before you used to move, now your movement is passed on to the worms you nourish. Once you grew nails and hair, now you’ll ooze slime which will make grass in the fields grow higher towards the sun. You will become grass, then milk for cows which will eat the grass, blood of the baby that drinks their milk, and so on. Don’t you see you get more out of life than I do, corpsey?”<br />...<br />Gurduloo, singing, began arranging to scoop out his corpse’s grave. He stretched it on the ground to take its measurement, marked the edges with his spade, moved it, and began digging at full speed. “Corpsey, maybe you’ll get bored waiting there.” He turned it over on a side, towards the grave, so as to keep it in view as he dug. “Corpsey, you might help with a spadeful or two yourself.” He straightened it up, tried to put in its hand a spade, which fell. “Enough. You’re not capable. I see I’ll have to dig it out myself, then you can fill the grave up.”<br />The grave was dug, but so messy was Gurduloo’s work that it turned out a strange irregular concave shape. Then Gurduloo decided to try it out. In he got and lay down. “Oh, how cosy it is, how comfy! What soft earth! How nice to turn over! Corpsey, do come and feel this lovely grave I’ve dug for you!” Then he thought a bit. “However, we’ve agreed that you must fill the grave, and it would be best if I stay down there, and you shovel the earth on me!” He waited a little, then, “Come on! Quick! It’s nothing! This is the way!” And from where he was lying down in the grave he began shovelling earth down by raising his spade. And the whole heap of earth fell down on top of him.<br />Agilulf and Raimbaut heard a muffled cry, whether of alarm or satisfaction at finding himself so well buried they did not know. They were just in time to extract Gurduloo, all covered with earth, before he died of suffocation.<br />The knight found Gurduloo’s work ill done and Raimbaut’s insufficient. He himself had traced out a whole little cemetery, marking the verges of rectangular graves, parallel to the two sides of an alley. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />A few years back I read the medieval The Book of the City of Ladies and made the same joke. But Italo Calvino beat me to it by *checks calendar* 50 years, though I was just being a dumbass, Calvino is funny: <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Agilulf was riding along, followed by Gurduloo. A damsel ran onto the road, with flowing hair and tattered dress, and flung herself on her knees. Agilulf stopped his horse. “Help, noble cavalier,” she invoked. “Half a mile from here a flock of wild bears is besieging the castle of my lady, the noble widow Priscilla. Only a few helpless women inhabit the castle. Nobody can get in or out. I was dropped by a rope from the battlements and escaped the claws of those beasts by a miracle. O knight, come and free us, do!”<br />...<br /><br />Agilulf came out onto the open space before the castle. Everything was black with bears. At the sight of horse and knight they bared their teeth and lined up side by side to bar his way. Agilulf set his lance and charged. One or two he pierced, others he stunned, others he bruised. Gurduloo came riding up and chased them with a kitchen spit. In ten minutes those not stretched on the ground like so many carpets had gone to hide in the forest depths.<br /><br /> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />A good anti-meditation joke, you don't get much of that anymore: <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[She invited him to lie down by her side. “They say,” said he, “that every night Cleopatra dreamt she had an armed warrior in her bed.”<br />“I’ve never tried,” she confessed, “they usually take it off beforehand.”<br />“Well, try now.” And slowly, without soiling the sheets, he entered the bed fully armed from head to foot and stretched out taut as if on a tomb.<br />“Don’t you even loosen the sword from its scabbard?”<br />“Amorous passion knows no half measures.” <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[“I am charged with the duty of relations with the profane. Words being often impure, the Knights prefer to abstain from them, and also to let the Grail speak through their lips.”<br />“Tell me what must I do to begin?”<br />“D’you see that maple leaf? A drop of dew has formed on it. Try and stand quite still and stare at the drop on that leaf, identify yourself with it, forget all the world in that drop, until you feel you have lost yourself and are pervaded by the infinite strength of the Grail.”<br />And he left him. Torrismund stared fixedly at the drop, stared and stared, began thinking of his own affairs, saw a frog jumping on the leaf, stared and stared at the frog, and then at the drop again, moved a foot which had gone numb, and then suddenly felt bored. In the woods knights appeared and disappeared, moving very slowly, their mouths open and eyes staring, accompanied by swans whose soft plumage they caressed every now and again. One suddenly threw wide his arms and with a hoarse cry broke into a little run.<br />“That one over there,” Torrismund could not prevent himself from asking the old man, who had reappeared nearby, “what’s up with him?”<br />“Ecstasy!” said the old man. “That is something you will never know, who are so distracted and curious. Those brothers have finally reached complete communion with the all.”<br />“And what about those?” asked the youth. Some knights were swaying about as if taken by slight shivers, and yawning.<br />“They’re still at an intermediate stage. Before feeling one with the sun and stars the novice feels as if he has the nearest objects within himself, very intensely. This has an effect, particularly on the youngest. Those brothers of ours whom you see are feeling a pleasant gentle tickle from the running brook, the rustling leaves, the mushrooms growing underground.”<br />“And don’t they tire of it in the long run?”<br />“Gradually they reach the higher states in which the nearest vibrations no longer occupy them but the great sweep of the skies, and very slowly they detach themselves from the senses.”<br />“Does that happen to all?”<br />“To few. And completely, only to one of us, the Elect, the King of the Grail.”<br />They had reached a glade where a large number of knights were exercising their arms before a canopied tribunal. Under that canopy was sitting or rather crouching, motionless, someone who seemed to be more mummy than man, dressed too in the uniform of the Grail, but more sumptuously. His eyes were open, indeed staring, in a face dried up as a chestnut.<br />“Is he alive?” asked the youth.<br />“He’s alive, but now he’s so rapt by love of the Grail that he no longer needs to eat or move or do his daily needs, or scarcely to breathe. He neither feels nor sees. No one knows his thoughts; they certainly reflect the movements of distant planets.”<br />“But why do they make him preside over military parades, if he doesn’t see?”<br />“’Tis a rite of the Grail.” <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>
  <a data-text-id="846763996503287109" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_2481056306" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2481056306" />
    <a title="Ahmed" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/50267833-ahmed"><img alt="Ahmed" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1485981265p2/50267833.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2481056306?book_show_action=true">Aug 04, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ahmed" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ahmed" href="/user/show/50267833-ahmed">Ahmed</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/19637099-the-nonexistent-knight">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2481056306" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17709773180535018551">There's something enchanting in the way Calvino writes. Far from matching the dazzling beauty of Invisible Cities, The Nonexistent Knight remains nothing short of a delightful read. Calvino, as if impersonating Monty Python, signs this lovely parody of the Medieval Age.</span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

    <a title="Ruth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11771264-ruth"><img alt="Ruth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593812370p2/11771264.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3101448391?book_show_action=true">Jan 14, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ruth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ruth" href="/user/show/11771264-ruth">Ruth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook 9781501963186" href="/book/show/51860744-the-nonexistent-knight">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11771264-ruth?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3101448391" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12196895564917462876">The Nonexistent Knight is an tale written by a cloistered nun- with secrets of her own, Sister Theodore writes:<br/>"Maybe this penance of mine has not been so ill chosen by the Mother Abbess. Every now and again I notice my pen beginning to hurry over the paper as if by itself, with my hurrying along after it. 'Tis towards the truth we hurry, my pen and I, the truth which I am constantly expecting to meet deep in a white page, and which I can reach only when my pen strokes have succeeded in burying </span>
  <span id="freeText12196895564917462876" style="display:none">The Nonexistent Knight is an tale written by a cloistered nun- with secrets of her own, Sister Theodore writes:<br />"Maybe this penance of mine has not been so ill chosen by the Mother Abbess. Every now and again I notice my pen beginning to hurry over the paper as if by itself, with my hurrying along after it. 'Tis towards the truth we hurry, my pen and I, the truth which I am constantly expecting to meet deep in a white page, and which I can reach only when my pen strokes have succeeded in burying all the disgust and dissatisfaction and rancor which I am forced here in seclusion to expiate. Then at<br /> a mere scamper of a mouse (the convent attics are full of them), or a sudden gust of wind banging the shutter (always apt to distract me, and I hurry to reopen it), or at the end of some episode in this tale and the start of another, or maybe just at the repetition of a line, my pen is heavy as a cross once again and my race towards truth wavers in its course. Now I must show the lands crossed by Agilulf and his squire on their journey."<br /> Agilulf Emo Bertrandin of the Guildivern and of the Others of Corbentraz and Sura, Knight of Selimpia Citeriore and Fez, is the nonexistent knight.<br />A paladin in Charlemagne's army, this paragon of a knight lives by a chivalric principles such as: honor, duty, propriety, loyalty, piety, bravery and order. Known for wearing an immaculately white suit of armor which is never removed because it is empty, Agiluif never exisited. <br />When Charlemagne learned of this asked Agiluif<br /> “Well, well! Who’d have thought it!” exclaimed Charlemagne. “And how do you do your job, then, if you don’t exist? <br />“By will power,” said Agiluif, “and faith in our holy cause!”<br /> When Aguluif's honor is called into question, his challenge becomes a quest sending him through France, England, and North Africa to confirm the chastity of a virgin, a daughter of the king of Scotland, he saved from rape 20 years earlier.<br /><br /> Never having read Italo Calvino's work, I was intrigued by the curious title and having understood the symbolism in this novella, I thought it to be more satirical than allegorical.<br />I have to say, I felt as though I fell into a literary rabbit's hole, it is the weirdest story I have ever read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12196895564917462876" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer2610939471968051632">The Nonexistent Knight is a story about Agilulf, an empty suit of armor, who is valiant and fights on Charlemagne’s behalf. At first, the story is quite puzzling. What would the purpose be in creating a hero without a body? Charlemagne himself is puzzled. In response to Charlemagne’s question on how Agilulf does his job without existing, he responds, “By will power and faith in our holy cause!”<br/><br/>The story is told by a nun, and relates her experience. Agilulf’s honor is challenged, which throws int</span>
  <span id="freeText2610939471968051632" style="display:none">The Nonexistent Knight is a story about Agilulf, an empty suit of armor, who is valiant and fights on Charlemagne’s behalf. At first, the story is quite puzzling. What would the purpose be in creating a hero without a body? Charlemagne himself is puzzled. In response to Charlemagne’s question on how Agilulf does his job without existing, he responds, “By will power and faith in our holy cause!”<br /><br />The story is told by a nun, and relates her experience. Agilulf’s honor is challenged, which throws into question the validity of his knighthood. He embarks on a quest to confirm that a women he believes he saved from rape was in fact a chaste virgin, and that he was not duped. I will avoid spoiling the final revelation. This story brings into question the nature and honesty of storytelling, and the basis of nobility and honor. Do we all play the roles expected of us in our suit of armor, or do we have inherent honor and substance within?<br /></span>
