<div id="review_2090802206" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2090802206" />
    <a title="Michael Haase" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36432305-michael-haase"><img alt="Michael Haase" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1498749694p2/36432305.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2090802206?book_show_action=true">Aug 12, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael Haase" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael Haase" href="/user/show/36432305-michael-haase">Michael Haase</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/19637099-the-nonexistent-knight">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2090802206" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4974324869439069098">This book has all of the comedic absurdity and pensive reflection that reader's of Calvino's fiction should come to expect. Though it's relatively short, it's brimming with interest and humor. The story progresses from one outlandish event to another, always keeping the reader surprised and never reaching a dull moment. Calvino is an absolute expert at wringing the maximum enjoyment from every scene, something which really shows in this novel. <br/><br/>There are only a few characters, but they each have </span>
  <span id="freeText4974324869439069098" style="display:none">This book has all of the comedic absurdity and pensive reflection that reader's of Calvino's fiction should come to expect. Though it's relatively short, it's brimming with interest and humor. The story progresses from one outlandish event to another, always keeping the reader surprised and never reaching a dull moment. Calvino is an absolute expert at wringing the maximum enjoyment from every scene, something which really shows in this novel. <br /><br />There are only a few characters, but they each have their quirks and distinctions, from the titular, non-existent knight to the ambitious and vengeful Rambaldo. It must be said however, that this is a tale of chilvary, meaning that the personae are rather static and one-dimensional, as the narrative focuses more on external actions rather than on character development. There's a comedic relief character in the novel named Gurdulu (not that the story needed a comedic relief to begin with) with which I honestly believe Calvino may have gone a bit overboard. This guy is more ridiculous than any other character I've seen in a Calvino story, and that's saying something. <br /><br />At a certain point, when all of the major characters split up and go their separate ways, the novel takes an abrupt dive into utter insanity. I don't want to spoil anything so I want try to describe it, but if you thought the story was ludicrous already, just wait until you reach this part. <br /><br />I also love the way the novel jumps back and forth between first and third-person, stepping outside of the narrative and giving the reader an outsider's commentary on the story, and the nature of fiction in general. In these portions Calvino waxes philosophical and in a way exposes himself, revealing a more personal side in his writing:<br /><br /><i>"Outside is high summer; from the valley rises a murmur of voices and a movement of water. My cell is high up and through its slit of a window I can see a bend of the river with naked peasant youths bathing, and further on, beyond a clump of willows, girls too have taken off their dresses and are going down to bathe. Now one of the youths has swum underwater and surfaced to look at them and they are pointing at him with cries. I might be there too, in gay company, with young folk of my own station, and servants and retainers...One starts off writing with a certain zest, but a time comes when the pen merely grates in dusty ink, and not a drop of life flows, and life is all outside, outside the window, outside oneself, and it seems that never more can one escape into a page one is writing, open out another world, leap the gap...Am I outside now? No, writing has not changed me for the better at all. I have merely used up part of my restless, conscienceless youth. What value to me will these discontented pages be?"</i><br /><br />These passages I found to be just simply beautiful.<br />This book is a fun and pleasant read, that is thought provoking but not in any way tedious. I recommend it to everyone.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4974324869439069098" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2193898262" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2193898262" />
    <a title="Mark Buchignani" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26258621-mark-buchignani"><img alt="Mark Buchignani" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442966385p2/26258621.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2193898262?book_show_action=true">Jul 31, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mark Buchignani" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mark Buchignani" href="/user/show/26258621-mark-buchignani">Mark Buchignani</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/36655622-the-non-existent-knight">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26258621-mark-buchignani?shelf=italo-calvino">italo-calvino</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2193898262" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10195442548078965786">In the third volume of his <i>Our Ancestors</i> trilogy, Italo Calvino offers a tale of the perfect knight – a very particular fellow, duty-bound, correct and proper, and a follower and enforcer of the rules to the letter: “Agilulf Emo Bertrandin of the Guildivern and of the Others of Corbentraz and Sura, Knight of Selimpia Citeriore and Fez was certainly a model soldier; but disliked by all.”<br/><br/>Why? Perhaps because he was nothing more than an unoccupied suit of armor.<br/><br/>Which sets the stage for the book’s a</span>
  <span id="freeText10195442548078965786" style="display:none">In the third volume of his <i>Our Ancestors</i> trilogy, Italo Calvino offers a tale of the perfect knight – a very particular fellow, duty-bound, correct and proper, and a follower and enforcer of the rules to the letter: “Agilulf Emo Bertrandin of the Guildivern and of the Others of Corbentraz and Sura, Knight of Selimpia Citeriore and Fez was certainly a model soldier; but disliked by all.”<br /><br />Why? Perhaps because he was nothing more than an unoccupied suit of armor.<br /><br />Which sets the stage for the book’s attitude of – of the emptiness of war, of the personalities and actions, of the posturing and bravado: of that which is put forward as required to compete in brutality and to win. This is the meaninglessness, therefore the futility, of war, and who better to expound, but the Non-Existent Knight.<br /><br />But when his credentials are questioned, off he goes to prove his worthiness, his accomplishments: he must find evidence of his elevating deed or lose his honor and his position at Charlemagne’s table – and cement his personal futility: the Non-Existent Knight himself would truly not be. Such is the commentary presented, and timeless it is: these events, maneuvers, and personages are in fact not. Denial of war! You can almost hear John Lennon singing, “All we are say-ing…”<br /><br />But after setting this stage, the author seems to grow bored of his premise. He skips over chunks of the story. Disinterest? This adds something of a Beowulf-esque feel to the narrative as the pages flip in slabs of time, until the conclusion tumbles up: Agilulf verifies his deed: he did in fact defend a virgin’s purity, and was therefore correctly elevated. And the crowning achievement comes when this uninhabited coat of mail makes love to a lonely widow who believes she has entrapped him. This he does skillfully, while avoiding the physical act (of which he is no doubt incapable). A nice finishing touch.<br /><br />And then – the protagonist disappears, rides off into the sunset as it were, leaving the secondary characters to clean up the mess as they can. Had Agilulf really been there at all? Or had this been one long metaphor for the moral life everyone should aspire to lead?<br /><br />There is a certain looseness about this final volume of <i>Our Ancestors</i>. Whereas the first two (<i>the Cloven Viscount</i>, and <i>the Baron in the Trees</i>) were complete in their telling, here Calvino seemed to tire, as if he turned to the end to see how it came out. That takes the book down a peg, that rush to finish.<br /><br />But was that the idea – a suit of armor devoid of a wearer and a novel with a hole in the middle? Maybe so. Hard to say.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10195442548078965786" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked

    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {

    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_28371223'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28371223-il-sentiero-dei-nidi-di-ragno-di-italo-calvino-una-favola-neorealista?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">&#39;Il sentiero dei nidi di ragno&#39; di Italo Calvino. Una favola neorealista<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/8250954.Marla_Rinwick?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Marla Rinwick<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.78 avg rating &mdash; 282 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published \n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer13968007992883593049\">Seminar paper from the year 2012 in the subject Romance Languages - Italian and Sardinian Studies, grade: 1,0, Free University of Berlin (Institut für Romanische Philologie), course: Il Neorealismo e gli anni sessanta, language: Italian, abstract: Pa<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText13968007992883593049\" style=\"display:none\">Seminar paper from the year 2012 in the subject Romance Languages - Italian and Sardinian Studies, grade: 1,0, Free University of Berlin (Institut für Romanische Philologie), course: Il Neorealismo e gli anni sessanta, language: Italian, abstract: Pavese (1951: 273) lo definiva “il più bel racconto che abbiamo sinora sull’esperienza partigiana” e Pedullà (cfr. 2006: XX) lo trova molto più interessante dei capolavori Uomini e no di Vittorini e L’Agnese va a morire di Viganò. Lo stesso autore desiderava non aver mai scritto quella “favola di bosco” (Pavese 1951: 274). Si parla di Italo Calvino, lo “scoiattolo della penna” (Pavese 1951: 273 sg.), e del suo primo romanzo Il Sentiero dei nidi di ragno, testo in cui egli elabora la sua esperienza nella Resistenza. Sebbene il libro riscosse un grande successo, Calvino in più riprese espresse il suo rammarico per aver raccontato la propria esperienza partigiana nella sua prima opera, e non averla conservata per la maturità e il suo ultimo libro. (cfr. Calvino 1964: 22 sg.) Il sentiero dei nidi di ragno fu il primo romanzo pubblicato in Italia sul tema della Resistenza (cfr. Eversmann 1979: 39). In un tempo in cui si tendeva a presentare la lotta partigiana in modo documentaristico ed eroico, Calvino fece un largo uso dell’ironia, fino a ridicolizzare le forze politiche che avevano contribuito alla liberazione italiana. Nella prefazione dell’edizione del 1964 l’autore stesso spiega i motivi di quella che all’epoca doveva essere sembrata solo una provocazione (cfr. Eversmann 1979: 47 sg.). In essa egli criticò i benpensanti, coloro che subito dopo la Liberazione si erano lamentati del comportamento, a volta delinquente, dei partigiani. In questo clima, quasi per dispetto, egli rappresentò i partigiani nel peggior modo possibile. [...] In questo lavoro analizzerò l’inizio di quella stagione romanziera, cioè l’opera Il Sentiero dei nidi di ragno. Prima descriverò il contesto storico della Resistenza, nel quale l’opera è ambientata, con i suoi avvenimenti principali, gli effetti che causò la lotta partigiana e l’impatto che ebbe sull’Italia. In seguito mi dedicherò alla corrente letteraria del Neorealismo, nella quale il romanzo di Calvino viene spesso inquadrato, spiegandone le caratteristiche, gli ideali e la cultura; poi descriverò come i neorealisti stessi valutavano la propria corrente letteraria. Dopo di ciò vorrei analizzare l’opera a partire dell’affermazione di Pavese (1951: 274), secondo il quale il romanzo è una “favola di bosco”, caratteristica che contraddice la sua catalogazione nella corrente neorealista. Infine prenderò in esame la critica ai partigiani che Calvino formula ne Il Sentiero.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"13968007992883593049\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer15769774439525545989">Italo Calvino was born in Cuba and grew up in Italy. He was a journalist and writer of short stories and novels. His best known works include the <i>Our Ancestors</i> trilogy (1952-1959), the <i>Cosmicomics</i> collection of short stories (1965), and the novels <i>Invisible Cities</i> (1972) and <i>If On a Winter's Night a Traveler</i> (1979).<br/><br/>His style is not easy to classify; much of his writing has an air reminiscent to th</span>
  <span id="freeText15769774439525545989" style="display:none">Italo Calvino was born in Cuba and grew up in Italy. He was a journalist and writer of short stories and novels. His best known works include the <i>Our Ancestors</i> trilogy (1952-1959), the <i>Cosmicomics</i> collection of short stories (1965), and the novels <i>Invisible Cities</i> (1972) and <i>If On a Winter's Night a Traveler</i> (1979).<br /><br />His style is not easy to classify; much of his writing has an air reminiscent to that of fantastical fairy tales (<i>Our Ancestors, Cosmicomics</i>), although sometimes his writing is more "realistic" and in the scenic mode of observation (<i>Difficult Loves</i>, for example). Some of his writing has been called <i>postmodern</i>, reflecting on literature and the act of reading, while some has been labeled magical realist, others fables, others simply "modern". He wrote: "<i>My working method has more often than not involved the subtraction of weight. I have tried to remove weight, sometimes from people, sometimes from heavenly bodies, sometimes from cities; above all I have tried to remove weight from the structure of stories and from language.</i>"<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15769774439525545989" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

From his perch in the trees, Cosimo sees the Age of Enlightenment pass by, and a new century dawn.\n \nThe Baron in the Trees exemplifies Calvino’s peerless ability to weave tales that sparkle with enchantment.
